<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="d8d2dff719e9406cb1b471026f90841b"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="d8d2dff719e9406cb1b471026f90841b">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'ec9497f7-1416-4497-af35-bd9711fa22c9'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        (function() {
            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {
                var store_domain = 'https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com';
                var session_id = '361B9DBD93CA86FD34BA8800794CA2A7';
                var url = ('https:' == document.location.protocol ? 'https://' : 'http://')
                    + "beacon.riskified.com?shop=" + store_domain + "&sid=" + session_id;
                var s = document.createElement('script');
                s.type = 'text/javascript';
                s.async = true;
                s.src = url;
                var x = document.getElementsByTagName('script')[0];
                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }
            if (window.attachEvent)
                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)
            else
                window.addEventListener('load', riskifiedBeaconLoad, false);
        })();
    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d46877995" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>An original Fe single atom doped MoS<sub>2</sub> hollow multishelled structure (HoMS) is designed for the first time, which promotes the electron transfer and Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetic, thereby achieving excellent rate capability. Besides, the strong adsorption with Na<sup>+</sup> and self-catalysis of Fe single atom facilitates the reversible conversion between 2H and 1T, and from Na<sub>x</sub>MoS<sub>2</sub> to Mo and Na<sub>2</sub>S, combining buffering effect of HoMS to improve the cyclic stability.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4d3ca8b3-2af4-4035-a29d-435c71beced9/anie202400285-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/4d3ca8b3-2af4-4035-a29d-435c71beced9/anie202400285-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/45686f31-45e4-4dc2-a708-46542709535c/anie202400285-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4d3ca8b3-2af4-4035-a29d-435c71beced9/anie202400285-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d46877998" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Low Na<sup>+</sup> and electron diffusion kinetics severely restrain the rate capability of MoS<sub>2</sub> as anode for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). Slow phase transitions between 2H and 1T, and from Na<sub>x</sub>MoS<sub>2</sub> to Mo and Na<sub>2</sub>S as well as the volume change during cycling, induce a poor cycling stability. Herein, an original Fe single atom doped MoS<sub>2</sub> hollow multishelled structure (HoMS) is designed for the first time to address the above challenges. The Fe single atom in MoS<sub>2</sub> promotes the electron transfer, companying with shortened charge diffusion path from unique HoMS, thereby achieving excellent rate capability. The strong adsorption with Na<sup>+</sup> and self-catalysis of Fe single atom facilitates the reversible conversion between 2H and 1T, and from Na<sub>x</sub>MoS<sub>2</sub> to Mo and Na<sub>2</sub>S. Moreover, the buffering effect of HoMS on volume change during cycling improves the cyclic stability. Consequently, the Fe single atom doped MoS<sub>2</sub> quadruple-shelled sphere exhibits a high specific capacity of 213.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at an ultrahigh current density of 30 A g<sup>−1</sup>, which is superior to previously-reported results. Even at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, 259.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (83.68 %) was reserved after 500 cycles. Such elaborate catalytic site decorated HoMS is also promising to realize other “fast-charging” high-energy-density rechargeable batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202400285-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202400285-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>MoS<sub>2</sub> is an outstanding candidate anode material for sodium ion batteries (SIBs) due to its moderate interlayer space of 0.62 nm for reversible intercalation/deintercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> ion,<span><sup><a href="#anie202400285-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0001_R_d46877987e621" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">1</a></sup></span> and high theoretical specific capacity of 670 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400285-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0002_R_d46877987e626" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Energy Mater.">2</a>-<a href="#anie202400285-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0004_R_d46877987e626" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a></sup></span> However, the sluggish electron and Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion kinetics lead to low specific capacity at high current density.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400285-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0005_R_d46877987e632" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">5</a>, <a href="#anie202400285-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0006_R_d46877987e632" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - J. Energy Chem.">6</a></sup></span> Especially, the large transition state energy barriers of conversion reaction between 2H and 1T, and from MoS<sub>2</sub> to Na<sub>2</sub>S and Mo,<span><sup><a href="#anie202400285-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0007_R_d46877987e639" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">7</a>-<a href="#anie202400285-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0009_R_d46877987e639" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Energy Mater.">9</a></sup></span> result in poor rate capability. Besides, the shuttle effect of sodium polysulfide produced by Na<sub>2</sub>S restrains the reversible electrochemical reaction,<span><sup><a href="#anie202400285-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0010_R_d46877987e645" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Mater.">10</a></sup></span> and the big volume variation during sodiation/desodiation process causes collapse of the structure, leading to poor cyclic stability.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400285-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0011_R_d46877987e648" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - ACS Nano">11</a>, <a href="#anie202400285-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0012_R_d46877987e648" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Chem. Eng. J.">12</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Rational nanostructure design has been proved fruitful in improving both the structure stability and reaction kinetics of materials. Wherein, hollow multishelled structure (HoMS) with multiple shells and interior cavities, has shown great promise in various application areas, including not only rechargeable batteries,<span><sup><a href="#anie202400285-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0013_R_d46877987e653" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Mater.">13</a>-<a href="#anie202400285-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0015_R_d46877987e653" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nano Res.">15</a></sup></span> but also electromagnetic wave absorption,<span><sup><a href="#anie202400285-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0016_R_d46877987e656" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Int. J. Min. Met. Mater.">16</a>, <a href="#anie202400285-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0017_R_d46877987e656" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Sci.">17</a></sup></span> catalysis,<span><sup><a href="#anie202400285-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0018_R_d46877987e659" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">18</a>-<a href="#anie202400285-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0020_R_d46877987e659" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">20</a></sup></span> sensor,<span><sup><a href="#anie202400285-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0021_R_d46877987e662" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Scr. Mater.">21</a>, <a href="#anie202400285-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0022_R_d46877987e662" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nat. Commun.">22</a></sup></span> and drug delivery,<span><sup><a href="#anie202400285-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0023_R_d46877987e665" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Nano Res.">23</a>, <a href="#anie202400285-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0024_R_d46877987e665" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Chem. Res. Chin. Univ.">24</a></sup></span> etc. Especially, as electrode materials for high energy density batteries, HoMS could effectively accommodate the volume change and the stress during charging-discharging process, thus improving the cyclic stability.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400285-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0025_R_d46877987e669" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">25</a>-<a href="#anie202400285-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0027_R_d46877987e669" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">27</a></sup></span> Additionally, it could shorten the charge diffusion path,<span><sup><a href="#anie202400285-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0028_R_d46877987e672" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Mater.">28</a>-<a href="#anie202400285-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0030_R_d46877987e672" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Nat. Chem. Rev.">30</a></sup></span> thus improving the rate capability.</p>
         
         <p>In addition to the structure engineering, the composition regulation has also shown great power in improving the performance.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400285-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0031_R_d46877987e678" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Energy Environ. Sci.">31</a>, <a href="#anie202400285-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0032_R_d46877987e678" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Energy Storage Mater.">32</a></sup></span> Typically, the strategy of decorating catalytic sites into the electrode material could significantly enhance the reaction kinetics, thus significantly improving the rate capability.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400285-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0033_R_d46877987e681" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Small">33</a>, <a href="#anie202400285-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0034_R_d46877987e681" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Rare Met.">34</a></sup></span> For example, the single Fe atom catalysts exhibit a strong chemical catalysis action for immobilizing the Na<sub>2</sub>S and promoting fast reversible conversion reaction from Mo and Na<sub>2</sub>S to MoS<sub>2</sub>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400285-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0035_R_d46877987e691" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Adv. Mater.">35</a></sup></span> Combing the structure design with composition regulation, one could look forward to simultaneously circumventing all the obstacles, and significantly improving both the cycling stability and rate capability. However, the synthesis of Mo-based HoMS, and synergetic control on both the structure and composition still remains a big challenge due to the preferential growth of (002) lattice plane.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">Herein, using a modified sequential templating approach (STA), Fe single atom doped MoS<sub>2</sub> HoMS (Fe−M-HoMS) was synthesized for the first time, via both the composition controlling and chemical etching. As shown in Scheme <a href="#anie202400285-fig-5001">1</a>a, firstly, Fe<sub>2</sub>(MoO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> HoMS (FMO-HoMS) was synthesized through STA by co-adsorption of FeCl<sub>3</sub> and (NH<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>MoO<sub>4</sub>. Then the MoS<sub>2</sub>/FeS<sub>2</sub> HoMS (MF-HoMS) was achieved after sulfuration according with the gas-phase reaction between S<sub>8</sub> and FMO-HoMS as shown in equation <a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#anie202400285-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>.
            <div class="inline-equation" id="anie202400285-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/87399b80-da30-4f3a-8a6c-9e2c9d7d76fd/anie202400285-math-0001.png"></span><math></math>
                  </span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
            
            <div class="inline-equation" id="anie202400285-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/3e081c55-b953-4571-b17b-7c9b4e216c79/anie202400285-math-0002.png"></span><math></math>
                  </span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
            </div>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202400285-fig-5001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/41c9e254-5c6e-43ba-9edb-e42f4a68a674/anie202400285-fig-5001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/41c9e254-5c6e-43ba-9edb-e42f4a68a674/anie202400285-fig-5001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d920f453-1b30-4012-9b69-540779f90e85/anie202400285-fig-5001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/41c9e254-5c6e-43ba-9edb-e42f4a68a674/anie202400285-fig-5001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Scheme 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202400285-fig-5001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202400285" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>The synthesis process and advantages of Fe−M-HoMS. (a) A modified sequential templating method following sulfuration and chemical etching were designed to fabricated Fe−M-HoMS. (b) Advantages schematic illustration of Fe−M-HoMS as anode of SIBs.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>And after a subsequent chemical etching treatment based on the reaction between FeS<sub>2</sub> and Fe<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> as shown in equation (2), the by-product FeS<sub>2</sub> was removed. We deduce that when the Fe<sup>3+</sup> contacts the exposed S on the surface of FeS<sub>2</sub>, the electron transfers from S to Fe<sup>3+</sup>, the redox reaction happens to form Fe<sup>2+</sup> and SO<sub>4</sub><sup>2−</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400285-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0036_R_d46877987e908" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Trans. Nonferrous Met. Soc. China">36</a></sup></span> Meanwhile, through calculations and literature references, it has been proven that thermodynamics is feasible. Then after the pyrolysis at high temperature of 800 °C, the Fe−M-HoMS with different shell numbers (Fe−M-HoMS−S, Fe−M-HoMS−D, Fe−M-HoMS−T, and Fe−M-HoMS−Q, wherein S, D, T and Q stand for single, double, triple and quadruple) was obtained. By tuning the synthetic conditions, the shell structure, shell porosity, lattice spacing of MoS<sub>2</sub> and Fe coordination structure, are well controlled. Exhilaratingly, this finely-tuned Fe−M-HoMS possesses abundant advantages as anode for SIBs. Depicted in Scheme <a href="#anie202400285-fig-5001">1</a>b, the Fe single atom in MoS<sub>2</sub> promotes the electron transfer, companying with shortened charge diffusion path as benefited from the unique HoMS to achieve fast electrochemical reaction for excellent rate capability. For one thing, the fast reversible conversion between 2H and 1T is facilitated due to the strong adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup> ion by Fe single atom. Additionally, the Fe single atom catalyzes the reversible reaction from Na<sub>x</sub>MoS<sub>2</sub> to Mo and Na<sub>2</sub>S. Moreover, HoMS buffers volume change during charging/discharging process, thus improving the cycling stability. As a result, Fe−M-HoMS exhibits superior rate capability and cycling stability when used as anode for SIBs. Especially, the Fe−M-HoMS−Q demonstrates the best rate capability, reaching a high specific capacity of 213.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at an ultrahigh current density of 30 A g<sup>−1</sup>, which compared to previous reports still displays a superior performance. Even at a high current density of 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, 259.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (83.68 %) was reserved after 500 cycles.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202400285-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202400285-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The structure and phase of the FMO-HoMS and MF-HoMS were systematically analyzed, as shown in Figure S1–5. The 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> phase (JCPDS#77–1716) of Fe−M-HoMS with (002) lattice spacing of 0.613 nm was observed in X-ray diffraction (XRD) pattern, and confirmed by the Raman spectrum and high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) (Figure S6–8 and S9a).<span><sup><a href="#anie202400285-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0037_R_d46877987e946" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Energy Mater.">37</a></sup></span> The Fe−M-HoMS demonstrated larger specific surface area and pore volume of 48.95 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> and 0.173 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure S10) than MF-HoMS. It should be ascribed to that more pores and exposed sites were generated via chemical etching based on the reaction between FeS<sub>2</sub> and Fe<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (Figure S11a-c and 1a, b). Nevertheless, Fe−M-HoMS well inherited the multiple-shelled spherical structure of FMO-HoMS, as detected clearly by SEM and TEM images (Figure S11d–f, S12). Wherein, the Fe<sup>2+</sup>, Mo<sup>4+</sup> and S<sup>2−</sup> were detected by XPS (Figure S9b–d),<span><sup><a href="#anie202400285-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0038_R_d46877987e973" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">38</a>, <a href="#anie202400285-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0039_R_d46877987e973" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Appl. Catal. B">39</a></sup></span> and according with the energy dispersive spectrometer (EDS), which shows that Mo and S, the Fe element was dispersed uniformly around Fe−M-HoMS (Figure <a href="#anie202400285-fig-0001">1c</a> and S13) with the atomic ratio of Fe being about 7.65 % (Table S1).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202400285-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a7c57954-6573-4e2c-98c3-f7c9c83dab15/anie202400285-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/a7c57954-6573-4e2c-98c3-f7c9c83dab15/anie202400285-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2dfdc3af-1124-4327-963e-8e1180e6b87a/anie202400285-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a7c57954-6573-4e2c-98c3-f7c9c83dab15/anie202400285-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202400285-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202400285" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Morphology and electronic structure of Fe−M-HoMS−Q. (a) SEM, (b) TEM, (c) element distribution, (d) low pass filtered atomic-resolution STEM–ADF and (e) enlarged part marked by the yellow dashed line in Figure <a href="#anie202400285-fig-0001">1d</a> of Fe−M-HoMS−Q. The Mo (f) k-edge XANES and (h) R space EXAFS analysis, the Fe (g) k-edge XANES and (i) R space EXAFS analysis of Fe−M-HoMS−Q.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To further explore the specific occupation of Fe single atom in MoS<sub>2</sub>, the atomic-resolution STEM–ADF imaging (Figure <a href="#anie202400285-fig-0001">1d</a>) were performed. No particles or clusters ascribed to metallic Fe or Fe compounds was detected, which demonstrated the existence of Fe single atom.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400285-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0040_R_d46877987e1013" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Mater.">40</a></sup></span> The observed single substitutional defect was due to the doping of Fe atom into MoS<sub>2</sub>. Meanwhile, the S (dimmer) and Mo (brighter) atoms were clarified clearly in the MoS<sub>2</sub> lattice because of the atomic number (Z) contrast shown in Figure <a href="#anie202400285-fig-0001">1e</a>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400285-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0041_R_d46877987e1024" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Mater.">41</a></sup></span> However, the Mo atoms, which should be ordered in the MoS<sub>2</sub> cellular atomic structure, become disordered. This phenomenon can be attributed to the doping of Fe single atoms.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400285-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0042_R_d46877987e1029" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Nano Lett.">42</a></sup></span> This phenomenon was further confirmed by the characteristic L2,3 edge of Fe atom in the atomic-scale STEM–EELS analysis (Figure S14). Additionally, the electronic structure information of Fe and Mo atoms were detected by the extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS) and X-ray absorption near-edge structure (XANES) spectrums, respectively. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202400285-fig-0001">1f</a>, the Mo k-edge XANES spectra of Fe−M-HoMS−Q was located at the middle of Mo file and MoS<sub>2</sub> standard, which demonstrated that the valence state of Mo was decreased due to the formation of defect caused by Fe atom doping. And the Fe k-edge XANES spectra of Fe−M-HoMS−Q (Figure <a href="#anie202400285-fig-0001">1g</a>) was near to FeS<sub>2</sub>, which depicts that the valence state of Fe includes +2 and +3. Meanwhile, compared to pure MoS<sub>2</sub>, the intensities of Mo−S and Mo−Mo in R-space EXAFS spectra of Fe−M-HoMS−Q (Figure <a href="#anie202400285-fig-0001">1h</a> and <a href="#anie202400285-fig-0001">1i</a>) were decreased, indicating the formation of defect and edge. Especially, no Fe−Fe bond was found, confirming the Fe atoms in Fe−M-HoMS were present as single-atom species.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400285-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0043_R_d46877987e1051" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - ACS Nano">43</a></sup></span> Furthermore, the changed coordination environment of Fe−M-HoMS was confirmed by the EXAFS spectra in K-space (Figure S15). Taking the metal foil and metal sulfide as standard state (Figure S16 and 17), the specific coordination number was further testified by fitting the R-space and K space EXAFS spectra, wherein, we further proved that the singe Fe atom coordinated with S and O as a ratio of 1/2 (marked as FeO4) depicted in Table S2. Therefore, one could conclude that the Fe single atom was doped into the lattice of MoS<sub>2</sub> in Fe−M-HoMS, which caused the formation of defect and change of coordination environment, leading to accelerated electrochemical reaction for excellent rate capability.</p>
         
         <p>The effect of Fe singe atom on electrochemical performance was explored, taking the pure MoS<sub>2</sub> particle (Figure S18) and MF-HoMS−Q as the comparisons. The rate capability was testified by galvanostatic charge–discharge at different current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, 5.0, 10.0, 20.0 30.0, and 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Obviously, compared to MoS<sub>2</sub> and MF-HoMS−Q, the Fe−M-HoMS−Q demonstrated higher specific capacities of 498.9, 414.4, 383.6, 357.7, 333.1, 300.0, 269.3, 239.8, 213.3, and 417.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at all the different current densities (Figure <a href="#anie202400285-fig-0002">2a</a>), which indicates a better rate capability. In addition, Fe−M-HoMS−Q achieved a higher initial specific capacity of 462.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at the fourth cycle with a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and a lower fading rate during circulation. A higher capacity was retained after 100 cycles (Figure <a href="#anie202400285-fig-0002">2b</a>), symbolizing a better cyclic stability.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202400285-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ca0a6606-bb22-485c-a8d6-712dbc9f1822/anie202400285-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ca0a6606-bb22-485c-a8d6-712dbc9f1822/anie202400285-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/cab791df-773e-4a59-91ac-bdad95c656cc/anie202400285-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ca0a6606-bb22-485c-a8d6-712dbc9f1822/anie202400285-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202400285-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202400285" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Electrochemical performance of Fe−M-HoMS in a half-cell system (a-g) and in a Fe−M-HoMS//NVP@C full-cell system (h-j). (a) The rate capacity at current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, 5.0, 10.0, 20.0, 30.0 and 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> and (b) cycling stability at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> of MoS<sub>2</sub>, MF-HoMS−Q and Fe−M-HoMS−Q. (c) Peak current density and voltage different between anode and cathode peaks in CV curves, (d) cycling stability at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and (e) rate capacity at current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, 5.0, 10.0, 20.0, 30.0 and 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> of Fe−M-HoMS. (f) Comparation of rate capability with other structural MoS<sub>2</sub> composite and (g) cycling stability at a current density of 5 A g<sup>−1</sup> of Fe−M-HoMS−Q. (h) The diagram of a Fe−M-HoMS//NVP@C full-cell, (i) CV curves at different scan rates and (j) rate capability of the full cell.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The effect of structure design on electrochemical reaction as anode for SIBs was investigated as well. Firstly, the electrochemical behavior was checked by cyclic voltammograms (CV) with a voltage window from 0.1 V to 2.8 V at a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. As shown in Figure S19, the cathode peaks of 0.59, 0.71 and 1.02 V were corresponded to the formation of SEI (solid electrolyte interface) and the following intercalation reaction at the initial discharge process, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400285-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0044_R_d46877987e1123" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Nano Energy">44</a></sup></span> The anode peaks of 1.38, 1.6, 2.28, and 2.58 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup> and corresponding cathode peaks of 0.15, 1.05, 1.4, 2.18 V and 2.30 V at the third cycle were attributed to the intercalation/deintercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> (above 0.4 V) and conversion reaction, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400285-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0007_R_d46877987e1130" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">7</a></sup></span> Meanwhile, the potential difference between oxidation and reduction peaks was decreased with increase of shell numbers, meaning a smaller polarization (Figure <a href="#anie202400285-fig-0002">2c</a> and S20).<span><sup><a href="#anie202400285-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0045_R_d46877987e1137" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Chem. Res. Chin. Univ.">45</a>, <a href="#anie202400285-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0046_R_d46877987e1137" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">46</a></sup></span> The peak current density was increased with the increase of shell number, signifying increased specific capacity. In addition, Fe−M-HoMS−Q exhibited the smallest charge-transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) between electrolyte and electrode materials (Figure S21). As confirmed by the galvanostatic discharge-charge measurement (Figure S22), the Fe−M-HoMS−Q demonstrated the highest specific capacity. Additionally, the Fe−M-HoMS−Q showed a good cycling stability. A specific capacity of 462.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at the fourth cycle with a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> was achieved, with 442 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> retained after 100 cycles (Figure <a href="#anie202400285-fig-0002">2d</a>). Although the specific capacity will decrease with increase of area loading, the cycling stability is excellent (Figure S23). Even at a high current density of 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, 259.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>(83.68 %) was reserved after 500 cycles (Figure <a href="#anie202400285-fig-0002">2g</a>). Furthermore, as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202400285-fig-0002">2e</a>, all the Fe−M-HoMS materials exhibited excellent rate capability. Especially, the Fe−M-HoMS−Q displayed a high specific capacity of 498.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and 213.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> was retained at an ultrahigh current density of 30 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure S24). When the current density was turned back to 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, a specific capacity of 417.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> was recovered, which demonstrated superb rate capability, and superior to most of reported results of MoS<sub>2</sub>-based anode materials (Figure <a href="#anie202400285-fig-0002">2f</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202400285-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0047_R_d46877987e1182" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Chem. Eng. J.">47</a>-<a href="#anie202400285-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0051_R_d46877987e1182" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Nano Res.">51</a></sup></span> Therefore, the Fe−M-HoMS−Q exhibited shorter Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion path for excellent rate capability and buffering volume expansion for outstanding cycling stability due to the unique HoMS structure.</p>
         
         <p>To further explore the practical value of Fe−M-HoMS−Q, a Fe−M-HoMS−Q//NVP@C full cell was assembled with home-made NVP@C<span><sup><a href="#anie202400285-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0052_R_d46877987e1190" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Chem. Res. Chin. Univ.">52</a>, <a href="#anie202400285-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0053_R_d46877987e1190" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Rare Met.">53</a></sup></span> as a cathode (Figure <a href="#anie202400285-fig-0002">2h</a>). The rate capability of full cell was checked by the CV test at different scan rates and the galvanostatic discharge-charge process at different current densities. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202400285-fig-0002">2i</a>, the faint change of peak position with increase of scan rate from 0.1 to 0.3 mV s<sup>−1</sup> certified outstanding rate capability. Additionally, a specific capacity of 129.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> based on cathode was achieved at a current density 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202400285-fig-0002">2j</a>), and still 55.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> retained at a high current density of 2 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Especially, when the current density switched back to 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup>, still 128.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> was achieved, meaning excellent rate capability. Simultaneously, the cycling stability of full cell at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> was performed (Figure S25), showing a good cyclic stability.</p>
         
         <p>Obviously, the Fe−M-HoMS−Q demonstrated a much better rate capability and cycling stability than the other samples, of which mechanism was explored in detail as follows. Firstly, the unique porous hollow quadruple-shelled structure with the largest specific surface area and pore volume (Figure S26) could provide multi-channel for permeation of electrolyte from surface to inner easily, which offers more active sites for redox reaction than other Fe−M-HoMS, shortens diffusion path of ion and electron, and buffers volume change during charging/discharging process. The ratio of capacitive capacity contribution can be calculated based on the CV curves with different scan rates (Figure S27).<span><sup><a href="#anie202400285-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0054_R_d46877987e1223" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Nano Lett.">54</a>-<a href="#anie202400285-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0056_R_d46877987e1223" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Chem. Res. Chin. Univ.">56</a></sup></span> As shown in Figure S28, the ratio of capacitive capacity contribution increased as the shell number increased, while Fe−M-HoMS−Q achieved the highest capacity contribution (Figure S29–S30). The largest capacity contribution resulted from the fastest Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetic which was confirmed by the largest Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient of 10<sup>−9.33</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> calculated by GITT test (Figure S31). Besides, Doping Fe single atom can improve the conductivity, which is proved by the DFT calculations. Three kinds of models with 2H or 1T phase (Figure S32), the MoS<sub>2</sub>, the Fe-MoS<sub>2</sub> in which one of Mo atom is replaced by Fe atom, the Fe−M-HoMS wherein the coordination environment of Fe is O and S as a ratio of 4/2, were constructed and optimized with the double-layer model of p(2x2) supercell. The energy of Fe−M-HoMS (−415.950 eV) was significantly lower than the pure MoS<sub>2</sub> (−415.448 eV) and Fe-MoS<sub>2</sub> (−409.268 eV), which proved the superior stability of Fe−M-HoMS configuration. The band gap of MoS<sub>2</sub>, Fe-MoS<sub>2</sub> and Fe−M-HoMS with 2H or 1T phase were calculated to be 1.173, 0.429, 0.191 or 1.171, 0.415, 0.131 eV (Figure <a href="#anie202400285-fig-0003">3a</a>). It was obvious that the introduction of Fe single atom decreased the band gap of both 2H and 1T MoS<sub>2</sub>, which facilitates transfer of electron.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400285-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0057_R_d46877987e1255" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Adv. Mater.">57</a></sup></span> Further combined with shortened Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion path benefited from HoMS, the fast electrochemical reaction is significantly improved to cast excellent rate capability.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202400285-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f1ac64a1-5b6c-4ac8-aa89-7318baba79b9/anie202400285-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/f1ac64a1-5b6c-4ac8-aa89-7318baba79b9/anie202400285-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4fadc4c8-ebe7-4ae6-b05e-9f1f04d08ad1/anie202400285-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f1ac64a1-5b6c-4ac8-aa89-7318baba79b9/anie202400285-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202400285-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202400285" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>The Density functional theory (DFT) calculations of MoS<sub>2</sub>, Fe-MoS<sub>2</sub> and Fe−M-HoMS. (a) DOS of 2H and 1T MoS<sub>2</sub>, Fe-MoS<sub>2</sub> or Fe−M-HoMS (FeO<sub>4</sub>). (b) Crystal structure and charge density difference of MoS<sub>2</sub> and Fe−M-HoMS inserted one Na<sup>+</sup> from side or top views. (c) Binding energy between MoS<sub>2</sub> and Na<sup>+</sup> or Fe−M-HoMS and Na<sup>+</sup> with increase of inserted Na<sup>+</sup> from one to nine. (d) Energy difference between 2H and 1T MoS<sub>2</sub> with zero, one or nine Na<sup>+</sup> inserted. (e) Lattice expansion degree of 2H MoS<sub>2</sub>, Fe-MoS<sub>2</sub> and Fe−M-HoMS with one or nine Na<sup>+</sup> inserted. (f) Lattice expansion degree of 1T MoS<sub>2</sub>, Fe-MoS<sub>2</sub> and Fe−M-HoMS with one or nine Na<sup>+</sup> inserted. (g) Formation energy from Mo and Na<sub>2</sub>S to Mo, Na and S, from Mo and Na<sub>2</sub>S to NaMoS<sub>2</sub> then to MoS<sub>2</sub> and Na, or from Mo, Na<sub>2</sub>S and Fe to NaMo<sub>1-x</sub>Fe<sub>x</sub>S<sub>2</sub> then to Fe-MoS<sub>2</sub> and Na.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Secondly, the Fe single atom promotes fast reversible conversion between 2H and 1T, and from Na<sub>x</sub>MoS<sub>2</sub> to Mo and Na<sub>2</sub>S. This deduction is proved by the DFT calculations applying the Vienna ab initio simulation package (VASP).<span><sup><a href="#anie202400285-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0058_R_d46877987e1352" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Phys. Rev. B">58</a>, <a href="#anie202400285-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0059_R_d46877987e1352" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Comput. Mater. Sci.">59</a></sup></span> The MoS<sub>2</sub>, Fe-MoS<sub>2</sub>, and Fe−M-HoMS models of inserted one Na<sup>+</sup> and nine Na<sup>+</sup> (Figure S33–34) were constructed and optimized with the models of double-layer model of p(2x2) supercell. In these models, the Na<sup>+</sup> only insert into one layer to study the changes of interlayer spacing and stability of different phase models. The charge density difference of optimized one Na<sup>+</sup> inserted MoS<sub>2</sub> and Fe−M-HoMS were shown in Figure <a href="#anie202400285-fig-0003">3b</a>. Meanwhile, the models of Figure <a href="#anie202400285-fig-0003">3b</a> adopt a fixed (ISIF=2) p(3x3) supercell with a vacuum of 15 Å. Compared to MoS<sub>2</sub>, in Fe−M-HoMS the electron tended to transfer from Na<sup>+</sup> to MoS<sub>2</sub>, demonstrating stronger interaction between Na<sup>+</sup> and MoS<sub>2</sub>. It signifies that the Na<sup>+</sup> is more likely to insert into Fe−M-HoMS compared to pure MoS<sub>2</sub>. This phenomenon is proved by the stronger binding energy between Na and Fe−M-HoMS than that between Na and MoS<sub>2</sub> (Figure <a href="#anie202400285-fig-0003">3c</a>) as well. Meanwhile, the energy difference between 2H and 1T MoS<sub>2</sub> (▵E=E<sub>1T</sub>–E<sub>2H</sub>) with zero, one or nine Na<sup>+</sup> insertion was clarified as shown Figure <a href="#anie202400285-fig-0003">3d</a>. Obviously, the energy difference in Fe−M-HoMS with one or nine Na<sup>+</sup> was lower than that in MoS<sub>2</sub> and Fe-MoS<sub>2</sub>. Compared to the energy difference in Fe−M-HoMS with zero and one, the energy difference in Fe−M-HoMS with nine was the lowest, meaning fast conversion between 2H and 1T MoS<sub>2</sub>. Thus, the existence of Fe single atom coordinated with S and O as a ratio of 2/4 (FeO<sub>4</sub>) could increase the interaction between Na<sup>+</sup> and MoS<sub>2</sub>, which promotes the MoS<sub>2</sub> (002) monolayer slip thus foster the fast conversion from 2H to 1T MoS<sub>2</sub> during discharging process. More interestingly, this phenomenon is more distinct with increase of inserted Na<sup>+</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400285-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0060_R_d46877987e1431" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Angew. Chem.">60</a></sup></span> Additionally, the stronger interaction between Na<sup>+</sup> and MoS<sub>2</sub> in Fe−M-HoMS attribute to Fe single atom brought smaller lattice expansion after inserting Na<sup>+</sup> than MoS<sub>2</sub> and Fe-MoS<sub>2</sub> (Figure <a href="#anie202400285-fig-0003">3e</a>, 3 f), which improved the structural stability during cycling. The detailed lattice parameters used in Figure <a href="#anie202400285-fig-0003">3e</a> and 3 f was shown in Table S3. Besides, the Fe single atom catalyzes the reaction between MoS<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>S, Mo as well. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202400285-fig-0003">3g</a>, the conversion energies from Mo and Na<sub>2</sub>S to Mo, Na and S, from Mo and Na<sub>2</sub>S to NaMoS<sub>2</sub> then to MoS<sub>2</sub> and Na, or from Mo, Na<sub>2</sub>S and Fe to NaMo<sub>1-x</sub>Fe<sub>x</sub>S<sub>2</sub> then to Fe-MoS<sub>2</sub> and Na were clarified. The lowest formation energy of Fe-MoS<sub>2</sub> originated from Mo, Na<sub>2</sub>S and Fe indicates that the existence of Fe single atom promotes the conversion between Mo, Na<sub>2</sub>S and MoS<sub>2</sub>. Therefore, the Fe single atom promotes fast reversible conversion between 2H and 1T, and from Na<sub>x</sub>MoS<sub>2</sub> to Mo and Na<sub>2</sub>S with smaller lattice expansion. Simultaneously, profit to the effect of HoMS structure to alleviate the volume expansion, the cycling stability has been further improved.</p>
         
         <p>The reversible electrochemical reaction was proved by in situ XRD and ex situ XPS analysis. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202400285-fig-0004">4a</a>, the 2H MoS<sub>2</sub> was detected at the initial stage. During discharging process, the diffraction peaks arise at 11.8° and 23.9° were attributed to the characteristic peaks of Na<sub>x</sub>MoS<sub>2</sub> originated from the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> into MoS<sub>2</sub> appeared, and its peak intensity increased accompanied with that of 2H MoS<sub>2</sub> decreased.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400285-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0061_R_d46877987e1513" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">61</a></sup></span> As discharging process continued, the Na<sub>2</sub>S and Mo appeared when the voltage was lower than 0.4 V,<span><sup><a href="#anie202400285-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400285-bib-0062_R_d46877987e1518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - Nano Res.">62</a></sup></span> corresponding to the conversion reaction from 2H MoS<sub>2</sub> to Mo and Na<sub>2</sub>S. During charging process, the peaks corresponding to Na<sub>2</sub>S and Mo disappeared gradually as well as the Na<sub>x</sub>MoS<sub>2</sub> when the voltage was higher than 1.0 V. Meanwhile, the 2H MoS<sub>2</sub> phase appeared again, meaning the reversible conversion reaction from Na<sub>2</sub>S and Mo to 2H MoS<sub>2</sub>. At the end of charging stage, the 1T MoS<sub>2</sub> generated, it gave side evidence that the existence of Fe single atom facilitated the reversible transformation of 2H and 1T MoS<sub>2</sub>. Furthermore, the ex situ XPS was performed to check the reversible electrochemical reaction of Fe−M-HoMS−Q. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202400285-fig-0004">4b</a>, the 2H MoS<sub>2</sub> was detected at the initial stage. During discharging process, the Na<sub>x</sub>MoS<sub>2</sub> was observed. When discharged to 0.3 V, Mo was detected, meaning the Na<sub>2</sub>S and Mo were generated due to the further reaction between Na<sub>x</sub>MoS<sub>2</sub> and Na<sup>+</sup> ion. During charging process, the peak corresponding to Na<sub>x</sub>MoS<sub>2</sub> recovered and then transform to 2H and 1T MoS<sub>2</sub>, indicating the highly-reversible conversion reaction for excellent cycling stability.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202400285-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8aa4b745-b089-4076-ae5c-181540c6ec42/anie202400285-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/8aa4b745-b089-4076-ae5c-181540c6ec42/anie202400285-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9cfc39e5-dff7-4351-bd38-89751ef58eca/anie202400285-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8aa4b745-b089-4076-ae5c-181540c6ec42/anie202400285-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202400285-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202400285" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>The characterization of reversible electrochemical reaction and the structure evolution during charging/discharging process of Fe−M-HoMS−Q. (a) In situ XRD analysis of Fe−M-HoMS−Q. (b) Ex situ XPS analysis of Fe−M-HoMS−Q. (c1-c5) Morphology evolution during discharging process characterized by in situ TEM. (d1-d5) Phase evolution during discharging process characterized by in situ SAED. (e) HRTEM at the initial stage of Fe−M-HoMS−Q. (f1) Corresponding vertical line intensity profiles of HRTEM image in Figure <a href="#anie202400285-fig-0004">4e</a>. (f2) Corresponding vertical line intensity profiles of HRTEM in Figure <a href="#anie202400285-fig-0004">4g</a>. (g) HRTEM at the final stage of Fe−M-HoMS−Q.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>In situ TEM was also applied to detect the structure evolution during discharging process. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202400285-fig-0004">4</a>c1, the multi-shelled structure was maintained well during the whole discharging process, while the volume increased gradually as the discharging continued (Figure <a href="#anie202400285-fig-0004">4</a>c2-4c4). At the final stage, the volume expansion rate of Fe−M-HoMS−Q was only 26.0 % (Figure <a href="#anie202400285-fig-0004">4</a>c5), which was lower than that of 99.6 % for MF-HoMS−Q (Figure S35a-S35e). it may be due to that the porous multi-shelled structure of Fe−M-HoMS−Q could better buffer volume expansion during sodiation process. Furthermore, the phase evolution was clarified by the in situ selected area electron diffraction (SAED). As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202400285-fig-0004">4</a>d1–d5, initially, the diffraction ring attributed to the (002) lattice plane of MoS<sub>2</sub> was detected, correspondingly a lattice space of 0.611 nm was recorded (Figure <a href="#anie202400285-fig-0004">4e</a> and 4f1). During discharging process, the lattice space was increased to 0.664 nm due to the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup>, accompanying with the appearance of Na<sub>2</sub>S, which leading to formation of tiny fragment product (Figure <a href="#anie202400285-fig-0004">4</a>f2—<a href="#anie202400285-fig-0004">4g</a>). However, compared to the amorphous form product of MF-HoMS−Q at the final stage of discharging process as shown in Figure S35h, the clear lattice plane signified the excellent lattice stability of Fe−M-HoMS−Q during discharging/charging process due to the unique Fe single atom doping.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202400285-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202400285-sec-0003-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In conclusion, Fe−M-HoMS is designed and synthesized for the first time through a modified sequential templating method, with both the composition controlling and chemical etching, which successfully addresses the above challenges. When used as anode material for SIBs, it exhibits both good cycling stability and superb rate capability. A high specific capacity of 213.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at an ultrahigh current density of 30 A g<sup>−1</sup> is achieved, which is superior to previously-reported results. Even at a high current density of 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, 259.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (83.68 %) was reserved after 500 cycles. Such good performance could be benefited from the synergistic effect of Fe single atom and MoS<sub>2</sub> HoMS, that's the Fe single atom in MoS<sub>2</sub> promotes the electron transfer, companying with shortened charge diffusion path as benefited from the unique HoMS to achieve fast electrochemical reaction for excellent rate capability. Meanwhile, the strong adsorption with Na<sup>+</sup> ion and self-catalysis of Fe single atom facilitates the fast reversible phase transitions. Additionally, HoMS buffers volume change, thus improving the cycling stability. This strategy provides an insight into constructing catalytic site decorated HoMS for rechargeable batteries with simultaneously improved rate capability and cycling stability.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202400285-sec-0004">
